<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GhazanConversionToIslam.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">Persian miniature</a> depicting <a href="/wiki/Ghazan" title="Ghazan">Ghazan</a>'s conversion from Buddhism to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the time of Genghis Khan, virtually every religion had found Mongol converts, from <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, while all religions were accepted in Mongol society. To avoid strife, Genghis Khan set up an institution that ensured complete religious freedom, though he himself was a <a href="/wiki/Tengrism" title="Tengrism">tengrist</a>. Under his administration, all religious leaders were exempt from taxation and from public service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initially there were few formal places of worship because of the nomadic lifestyle. However, under <a href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei_Khan" title="Ögedei Khan">Ögedei</a> (1186–1241), several building projects were undertaken in the Mongol capital of <a href="/wiki/Karakorum" title="Karakorum">Karakorum</a>. Along with palaces, Ögedei built houses of worship for the Buddhist, Muslim, Christian, and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoist</a> followers. The dominant religions at that time were <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">Shamanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tengrism" title="Tengrism">Tengrism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, although Ögedei's wife was a Nestorian Christian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup> Eventually, each of the successor states adopted the dominant religion of the local populations: the Chinese-Mongolian <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> in the East (originally the great khan's domain) embraced <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, while the three Western khanates (the Central Asian <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khanate" title="Chagatai Khanate">Chagatai Khanate</a>, the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> and the Eastern European <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a>) adopted <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foltz._pp._105.E2.80.93106_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foltz._pp._105.E2.80.93106-127">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts_and_literature">Arts and literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mongol_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Arts and literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_historical_cities_and_towns_of_Mongolia" title="List of historical cities and towns of Mongolia">List of historical cities and towns of Mongolia</a></div>
<p>The oldest surviving literary work in the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian language</a> is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a></i>, which was written for the royal family some time after Genghis Khan's death in 1227. It is the most significant native account of Genghis's life and genealogy, covering his origins and childhood through to the establishment of the Mongol Empire and the reign of his son, Ögedei.</p>
<p>Another classic from the empire is the <i><a href="/wiki/Jami%27_al-tawarikh" title="Jami' al-tawarikh">Jami' al-tawarikh</a></i>, or "Universal History". It was commissioned in the early 14th century by the Ilkhan <a href="/wiki/Abaqa_Khan" title="Abaqa Khan">Abaqa Khan</a> as a way of documenting the entire world's history, to help establish the Mongols' own cultural legacy.</p>
<p>Mongol scribes in the 14th century used a mixture of resin and vegetable pigments as a primitive form of <a href="/wiki/Correction_fluid" title="Correction fluid">correction fluid</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup> this is arguably its first known usage.</p>
<p>The Mongols also appreciated the visual arts, though their taste in portraiture was strictly focused on portraits of their horses, rather than of people.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mail_system">Mail system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mongol_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Mail system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Yam_(route)" title="Yam (route)">Yam (route)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg"><img alt="A partially unrolled scroll, opened from left to right to show a portion of the scroll with widely spaced vertical lines in the Mongol language. Imprinted over two of the lines is an official-looking square red stamp with an intricate design." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1519" data-file-width="1785" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg/220px-OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg/330px-OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg/440px-OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 1305 letter (on a scroll measuring 302 by 50 centimetres (9.91 by 1.64 ft)) from the Ilkhan Mongol <a href="/wiki/%C3%96ljait%C3%BC" title="Öljaitü">Öljaitü</a> to King <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Mongol Empire had an ingenious and efficient mail system for the time, often referred to by scholars as the <a href="/wiki/Yam_(route)" title="Yam (route)">Yam</a>, which had lavishly- furnished and well-guarded relay posts known as <i>örtöö</i> setup all over the Empire. The <i>yam</i> system would be replicated later in the United States, in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Pony_Express" title="Pony Express">Pony Express</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chambers_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chambers-131">[128]</a></sup> A messenger would typically travel 25 miles (40 km) from one station to the next, either receiving a fresh, rested horse, or relaying the mail to the next rider to ensure the speediest possible delivery. The Mongol riders regularly covered 125 miles (200 km) per day, better than the fastest record set by the Pony Express some 600 years later.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Genghis and his successor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei" title="Ögedei">Ögedei</a> built a wide system of roadways, one of which carved the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altai_Range" title="Altai Range">Altai Range</a>. After his enthronement, Ögedei further organized the road system, ordering the Chagatai Khanate and Golden Horde to link up roads in western parts of the Mongol Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup> In order to reduce pressure on households, he set up relay stations with attached households every 25 miles (40 km). Anyone with <a href="/wiki/Paiza" title="Paiza">paiza</a> was allowed to stop there for re-mounts and specified rations, while those carrying military identities used the Yam even without a paiza. When the great khan died in Karakorum, news reached the Mongol forces under <a href="/wiki/Batu_Khan" title="Batu Khan">Batu Khan</a> in Central Europe within 4–6 weeks thanks to the Yam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weatherford._p._158_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weatherford._p._158-57">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>, built special relays for high officials, as well as ordinary relays that had hostels. During Kublai's reign, the Yuan communication system consisted of some 1,400 postal stations, which used 50,000 horses, 8,400 oxen, 6,700 mules, 4,000 carts, and 6,000 boats.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, the Mongols still used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dogsled" title="Dogsled">dogsled</a> relays for the yam. In the Ilkhanate, Ghazan restored the declining relay system in the Middle East on a restricted scale. He constructed some hostels and decreed that only imperial envoys could receive a stipend. The Jochids of the Golden Horde financed their relay system by a special yam tax.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Silk_Road">Silk Road</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mongol_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Silk Road">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Pax Mongolica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5612" data-file-width="3683" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg/170px-Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg/255px-Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg/340px-Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuda_Mengu" title="Tuda Mengu">Tuda Mengu</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Mongols had a history of supporting merchants and trade. Genghis Khan had encouraged foreign merchants early in his career, even before uniting the Mongols. Merchants provided him with information about neighboring cultures, served as diplomats and official traders for the Mongols, and were essential for many needed goods, since the Mongols produced little of their own.</p>
<p>Mongols sometimes provided capital for merchants and sent them far afield, in an <i>ortoq</i> (merchant partner) arrangement. As the empire grew, any merchants or ambassadors with proper documentation and authorization received protection and sanctuary as they traveled through Mongol realms. Well-traveled and relatively well-maintained roads linked lands from the Mediterranean basin to China, greatly increasing overland trade and resulting in some dramatic stories of those who travelled through what would become known as the Silk Road.</p>
<p>Western explorer <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> traveled east along the Silk Road, and the Chinese Mongol monk <a href="/wiki/Rabban_Bar_Sauma" title="Rabban Bar Sauma">Rabban Bar Sauma</a> made a comparably epic journey along the route, venturing from his home of <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq</a> (Beijing) as far as Europe. European missionaries, such as <a href="/wiki/William_of_Rubruck" title="William of Rubruck">William of Rubruck</a>, also traveled to the Mongol court to convert believers to their cause, or went as papal envoys to correspond with Mongol rulers in an attempt to secure a <a href="/wiki/Franco-Mongol_alliance" title="Franco-Mongol alliance">Franco-Mongol alliance</a>. It was rare, however, for anyone to journey the full length of Silk Road. Instead, merchants moved products like a bucket brigade, goods being traded from one middleman to another, moving from China all the way to the West; the goods moving over such long distances reached extravagant prices.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%88%90%E5%90%89%E6%80%9D%E6%B1%97%E9%87%91%E5%B8%81.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/%E6%88%90%E5%90%89%E6%80%9D%E6%B1%97%E9%87%91%E5%B8%81.JPG/220px-%E6%88%90%E5%90%89%E6%80%9D%E6%B1%97%E9%87%91%E5%B8%81.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/%E6%88%90%E5%90%89%E6%80%9D%E6%B1%97%E9%87%91%E5%B8%81.JPG/330px-%E6%88%90%E5%90%89%E6%80%9D%E6%B1%97%E9%87%91%E5%B8%81.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/%E6%88%90%E5%90%89%E6%80%9D%E6%B1%97%E9%87%91%E5%B8%81.JPG/440px-%E6%88%90%E5%90%89%E6%80%9D%E6%B1%97%E9%87%91%E5%B8%81.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%88%90%E5%90%89%E6%80%9D%E6%B1%97%E9%87%91%E5%B8%81.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coin</a> inscribed in the name of <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Genghis, the merchant partner business continued to flourish under his successors Ögedei and Güyük. Merchants brought clothing, food, information, and other provisions to the imperial palaces, and in return the great khans gave the merchants tax exemptions and allowed them to use the official relay stations of the Mongol Empire. Merchants also served as tax farmers in China, Russia and Iran. If the merchants were attacked by bandits, losses were made up from the imperial treasury.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Policies changed under the Great Khan <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Khan" title="Möngke Khan">Möngke</a>. Because of money laundering and overtaxing, he attempted to limit abuses and sent imperial investigators to supervise the <i>ortoq</i> businesses. He decreed all merchants must pay commercial and property taxes, and he paid off all drafts drawn by high-ranking Mongol elites from the merchants. This policy continued in the Yuan dynasty.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The fall of the Mongol Empire in the 14th century led to the collapse of the political, cultural, and economic unity along the Silk Road. Turkic tribes seized the western end of the route from the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, sowing the seeds of a Turkic culture that would later crystallize into the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> faith. In the East, the native Chinese overthrew the Yuan dynasty in 1368, launching their own <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> and pursuing a policy of economic isolationism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footsteps_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-footsteps-133">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mongol_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mongolia" title="History of Mongolia">History of Mongolia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mongols-map.png"><img alt="Map of Asia" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="728" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Mongols-map.png/220px-Mongols-map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Mongols-map.png/330px-Mongols-map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Mongols-map.png/440px-Mongols-map.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mongols-map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing the boundary of 13th century Mongol Empire compared to today's Mongols in <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, Russia, the <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asian</a> States, and China</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Mongol Empire — at its height the largest contiguous empire in history — had a lasting impact, unifying large regions. Some of these (such as eastern and western Russia and the western parts of China) remain unified today, albeit under different rulership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup> Mongols other than the main population might have been assimilated into local populations after the fall of the empire, and some of these descendants adopted local <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religions</a> — for example, the eastern khanate largely adopted <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, and the three western khanates adopted <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, largely under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foltz._pp._105.E2.80.93106_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foltz._pp._105.E2.80.93106-127">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Statement needs to be more specific about the content to which it refers. (February 2012)">specify</span></a></i>]</sup> interpretations, Genghis Khan's conquests caused wholesale destruction on an unprecedented scale in certain geographical regions, leading to changes in the demographics of Asia, such as the mass migration of the Iranian tribes of Central Asia into modern-day Iran. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a> was also subject to massive changes as a result of Mongol invasions. The population of the Iranian plateau suffered from widespread disease and famine, resulting in the deaths of up to three-quarters of its population, possibly 10 to 15 million people. Historian Steven Ward estimates that Iran's population did not again reach its pre-Mongol levels until the mid-20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steve_Ward_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steve_Ward-135">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Non-military achievements of the Mongol Empire included the introduction of a writing system, a Mongol alphabet based on <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_language" title="Uyghur language">Uyghur language</a> characters, that is still used today in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10,_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="796" data-file-width="438" height="400" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg/220px-Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg/330px-Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10,_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Tokhtamysh" title="Tokhtamysh">Tokhtamysh</a> and the armies of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a> initiate the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Moscow_(1382)" title="Siege of Moscow (1382)">Siege of Moscow (1382)</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some of the other long-term consequences of the Mongol Empire include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Moscow rose to prominence during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Tataro-Mongol_Yoke_in_Russia" title="Timeline of the Tataro-Mongol Yoke in Russia">Mongol-Tatar yoke</a>, some time after Russian rulers were accorded the status of tax collectors for the Mongols. The fact that the Russians collected tribute and taxes for the Mongols meant that the Mongols themselves would rarely visit the lands that they owned. The Russians eventually gained military power, and their ruler <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III_of_Russia" title="Ivan III of Russia">Ivan III</a> overthrew the Mongols completely to form the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_principality" title="Moscow principality">Russian Tsardom</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Great_stand_on_the_Ugra_river" title="Great stand on the Ugra river">Great stand on the Ugra river</a> proved the Mongols vulnerable, the Grand Duke of Moscow gained independence.</li>
<li>Europe's knowledge of the known world was immensely expanded by the information brought back by ambassadors and merchants. When <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Columbus</a> sailed in 1492, his missions were to reach <a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a>, the land of the Grand Khan in China, and give him a letter from the monarchs <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">Ferdinand II of Aragon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Isabella I of Castile</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>Some studies indicate that the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> that devastated Europe in the late 1340s may have traveled from China to Europe along the trade routes of the Mongol Empire. In 1347, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoese</a> possessor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feodosiya" title="Feodosiya">Caffa</a>, a great trade emporium on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimean_Peninsula" title="Crimean Peninsula">Crimean Peninsula</a>, came under siege by an army of Mongol warriors under the command of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Janibeg" title="Janibeg">Janibeg</a>. After a protracted siege during which the Mongol army was reportedly withering from the disease, they decided to use the infected corpses as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_weapon" title="Biological weapon">biological weapon</a>. The corpses were catapulted over the city walls, infecting the inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup> The Genoese traders fled, transferring the plague via their ships into the south of Europe, whence it rapidly spread. The total number of deaths worldwide from the pandemic is estimated at 75 million people, with an estimated 20 million deaths in Europe alone.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meczennicy_Sandomierscy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="330" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Meczennicy_Sandomierscy.jpg/170px-Meczennicy_Sandomierscy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Meczennicy_Sandomierscy.jpg/255px-Meczennicy_Sandomierscy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/Meczennicy_Sandomierscy.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meczennicy_Sandomierscy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dominican martyrs killed by Mongols during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Mongol_invasion_of_Poland" title="Second Mongol invasion of Poland">Mongol invasion of Poland</a> in 1260.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Western researcher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R._J._Rummel" title="R. J. Rummel">R. J. Rummel</a> estimated that 30 million people were killed under the rule of the Mongol Empire. This is a low-end estimate, as some estimates go as high as 80 million, with 50 million deaths being the middle ground. The population of China fell by half in fifty years of Mongol rule. Before the Mongol invasion, the territories of the Chinese dynasties reportedly had approximately 120 million inhabitants; after the conquest was completed in 1279, the 1300 census reported roughly 60 million people. While it is tempting to attribute this major decline solely to Mongol ferocity, scholars today have mixed opinions regarding this subject. Scholars such as Frederick W. Mote argue that the wide drop in numbers reflects an administrative failure to record rather than a <i>de facto</i> decrease, whilst others such as Timothy Brook argue that the Mongols reduced much of the south Chinese population, and very debatably of the Han Chinese population, to an invisible status through cancellation of the right to passports and denial of the right to direct land ownership. This meant that the Chinese had to depend on and be cared for chiefly by Mongols and Tartars, which also involved recruitment into the Mongol army. Other historians such as William McNeill and David Morgan argue that the Bubonic Plague was the main factor behind the demographic decline during this period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>David Nicole states in <i>The Mongol Warlords</i>, "terror and mass extermination of anyone opposing them was a well tested Mongol tactic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mongol_Conquests_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mongol_Conquests-138">[135]</a></sup> About half of the Russian population may have died during the invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parallelsixty.com_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-parallelsixty.com-139">[136]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Colin_McEvedy" title="Colin McEvedy">Colin McEvedy</a> (<i>Atlas of World Population History, 1978</i>) estimates the population of Russia-in-Europe dropped from 7.5 million prior to the invasion to 7 million afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mongol_Conquests_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mongol_Conquests-138">[135]</a></sup> Historians estimate that up to half of Hungary's two million population at that time were victims of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Europe" title="Mongol invasion of Europe">Mongol invasion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup> Historian Andrea Peto says that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rogerius_of_Apulia" title="Rogerius of Apulia">Rogerius</a>, an eyewitness, said "the Mongols killed everybody regardless of gender or age" and that "the Mongols especially 'found pleasure' in humiliating women."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Babur_and_Humayun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="444" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Babur_and_Humayun.jpg/220px-Babur_and_Humayun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Babur_and_Humayun.jpg/330px-Babur_and_Humayun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Babur_and_Humayun.jpg/440px-Babur_and_Humayun.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Babur_and_Humayun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_Emperor" title="Mughal Emperor">Mughal Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> and his heir <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>One of the more successful tactics employed by the Mongols was to wipe out urban populations that refused to surrender. In the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Rus%27" title="Mongol invasion of Rus'">Mongol invasion of Rus'</a>, almost all major cities were destroyed. If they chose to submit, the people were generally spared, though this was not guaranteed. For example, the city of Hamadan in modern-day Iran was destroyed and every man, woman, and child executed by Mongol general Subadai, after surrendering to him but failing to have enough provisions for his Mongol scouting force. Several days after the initial razing of the city, Subadai sent a force back to the burning ruins and the site of the massacre to kill any inhabitants of the city who had been away at the time of the initial slaughter and had returned in the meantime. Mongolian armies made use of local peoples and their soldiers, often incorporating them into their armies. Prisoners of war sometimes were given the choice between death and becoming part of the Mongol army to aid in future conquests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[139]</a></sup> In addition to intimidation tactics, the rapid expansion of the empire was facilitated by military hardiness (especially during bitterly cold winters), military skill, meritocracy, and discipline.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kalmyk_exodus_(Geoffroy,_1845).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="627" data-file-width="902" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Kalmyk_exodus_%28Geoffroy%2C_1845%29.JPG/220px-Kalmyk_exodus_%28Geoffroy%2C_1845%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Kalmyk_exodus_%28Geoffroy%2C_1845%29.JPG/330px-Kalmyk_exodus_%28Geoffroy%2C_1845%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Kalmyk_exodus_%28Geoffroy%2C_1845%29.JPG/440px-Kalmyk_exodus_%28Geoffroy%2C_1845%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kalmyk_exodus_(Geoffroy,_1845).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Kalmyk migration from Russia to China in 1770–1771</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Khanate" title="Crimean Khanate">Crimean Khanate</a> and other descendants, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> royal family of South Asia, are also descended from Genghis Khan: <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a>'s mother was a descendant — whereas his father was directly descended from <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> (Tamerlane). The word <i>"Mughol"</i> is a Persian word for Mongol.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Kalmyks" title="Kalmyks">Kalmyks</a> were the last Mongol nomads to penetrate European territory, having migrated to Europe from Central Asia at the turn of the 17th century. In the winter of 1770–1771, approximately 200,000 Kalmyks began the journey from their pastures on the left bank of the <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" title="Volga River">Volga River</a> to <a href="/wiki/Dzungaria" title="Dzungaria">Dzungaria</a>, through the territories of their <a href="/wiki/Kazakhs" title="Kazakhs">Kazakh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_people" title="Kyrgyz people">Kyrgyz</a> enemies. After several months of travel, only one-third of the original group reached Dzungaria in northwest China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li>Some Turko-Mongol Khanates lasted into recent centuries: The Crimean Khanate lasted until 1783; the <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Bukhara" title="Khanate of Bukhara">Khanate of Bukhara</a> until 1785; the <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_Khanate" title="Kazakh Khanate">Kazakh Khanate</a> until 1847; the <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Kokand" title="Khanate of Kokand">Khanate of Kokand</a> until 1876; and the <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Khiva" title="Khanate of Khiva">Khanate of Khiva</a> survived under a Russian protectorate until 1917.</li>
</ul>

<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Karakorum" title="Karakorum">Karakorum</a> was founded in 1220 and served as capital from 1235 to 1260.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Following the death of <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Khan" title="Möngke Khan">Möngke Khan</a> in 1259, no one city served as capital. <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq</a> (Dadu), modern-day <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, was the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan</a> capital between 1271 and 1368.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Including coins such as <a href="/wiki/Dirham" title="Dirham">dirhams</a> and paper currencies based on silver (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sukhe_(unit)" title="Sukhe (unit)">sukhe</a></i>) or silk, or the later <a href="/wiki/Chao_(currency)" title="Chao (currency)">Chao</a> currency of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

New York, USA: Three Rivers Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-609-80964-4" title="Special:BookSources/0-609-80964-4">0-609-80964-4</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMongol+Empire&amp;rft.au=Weatherford%2C+Jack&amp;rft.btitle=Genghis+Khan+and+the+Making+of+the+Modern+World&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-609-80964-4&amp;rft.place=New+York%2C+USA&amp;rft.pub=Three+Rivers+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
